    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer18288042707474134876">A man returns to the town where a baffling murder took place twenty-seven years earlier, determined to get to the bottom of the story. Just hours after marrying the beautiful Angela Vicario, everyone agrees, Bayardo San Roman returned his bride in disgrace to her parents. Her distraught family forced her to name her first lover; and her twin brothers announced their intent</span>
  <span id="freeText18288042707474134876" style="display:none">A man returns to the town where a baffling murder took place twenty-seven years earlier, determined to get to the bottom of the story. Just hours after marrying the beautiful Angela Vicario, everyone agrees, Bayardo San Roman returned his bride in disgrace to her parents. Her distraught family forced her to name her first lover; and her twin brothers announced their intention to murder Santiago Nasar for dishonoring their sister.<br /><br />Yet if everyone knew the murder was going to happen, why did no one intervene to try and stop it? The more that is learned, the less is understood, and as the story races to its inexplicable conclusion, an entire society--not just a pair of murderers—is put on trial.</span>

<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/31006810-v-a-court-of-wings-and-ruin-is-new-adult-erotica-but-goodreads-editors"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1496622329i/31006810._UX100_CR0,18,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/31006810-v-a-court-of-wings-and-ruin-is-new-adult-erotica-but-goodreads-editors">V. A Court of Wings and Ruin is NEW ADULT/EROTICA but Goodreads editors won&#39;t tell you to include it in the choice awards</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Considering the misogynism of the author I think he was ready to doubt that a rape victim was telling the truth. Abuse victims don't come forward beca<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Considering the misogynism of the author I think he was ready to doubt that a rape victim was telling the truth. Abuse victims don't come forward because they know that nobody will believe them. Garcia Marques immediatly assumed that she was trying to cover a lover. Because in rape-culture a woman is always to blame<br /><br />Remember that Santiago was ready to rape the Divina Flore the little black UNDERAGE girl that lived in his house like his father did before with Divina's mother. Remember that he got drunk a lot and that he liked to use women. Wouldn't a man like that rape Angela during a party in their teens and then forget about it because he was so drunk? <br /><br />Just as Garcia Marques is predisposed to doubt women I'm predisposed to believe rape victims. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">no. if you are learning spanish, perhaps you should read a book that is simpler to understand. I read the book, and as a native spanish peaker i can s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">no. if you are learning spanish, perhaps you should read a book that is simpler to understand. I read the book, and as a native spanish peaker i can say that i was a bit confused. The story is like one of the movies where nothing makes sense until the end, and you have to follow the narrative closely so as not to get lost. It is an excellent book, just not for that purpose.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_2846974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2846974" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/163833-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2846974?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/163833-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2846974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1451239356578169349">I own about 70 copies of illegally-photocopied versions of this book so I can use it with my students in class. And unlike most books I teach, I read this one every year.<br/><br/>Why? 'Cuz it's an unbelievable text.<br/><br/>I firmly believe that Santiago Nasar is one of literature's greatest Christ-figures. Many of Garcia's books have Christ-figures, of course, but Santiago is Jesus with a twist. When the book starts, Santiago is portrayed as a bad man who is wasteful and immoral and violent. When he is fingered </span>
  <span id="freeText1451239356578169349" style="display:none">I own about 70 copies of illegally-photocopied versions of this book so I can use it with my students in class. And unlike most books I teach, I read this one every year.<br /><br />Why? 'Cuz it's an unbelievable text.<br /><br />I firmly believe that Santiago Nasar is one of literature's greatest Christ-figures. Many of Garcia's books have Christ-figures, of course, but Santiago is Jesus with a twist. When the book starts, Santiago is portrayed as a bad man who is wasteful and immoral and violent. When he is fingered for taking the virginity of a non-whore, any reader would believe in his guilt.<br /><br />But by the end of the book, it is obvious that Santiago did not commit the crime (Garcia hides the line where this is stated unequivocally, but it is there). But he is killed nonetheless (multiple times, actually) and the entire town, who did not stop the attack on Santiago, feels guilty for their inaction for the next twenty years.<br /><br />It's such a brilliant idea that is told so exquisitely, that I can't think of a better sub-100 page book to read over a two day period. And my students can't, either, as I have never had a disappointed student. Ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1451239356578169349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2871203749" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2871203749" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2871203749?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=spanish">spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=colombian-authors">colombian-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2871203749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9569106783632378008">Truth in the title: the title tells us exactly the plot of the novel. <br/><br/>A man is stabbed to death. He’s a fairly wealthy young man (he’s 21) who runs a cattle ranch inherited from his father. There’s an interesting ethnic twist: His father’s family speaks Arabic at home and his Spanish mother on occasion calls him “white man.” Although the locals call his family ‘Turks,’ because they are Catholic, it’s likely they were Lebanese and arrived in Colombia (where the story is set) from the great mixing</span>
  <span id="freeText9569106783632378008" style="display:none">Truth in the title: the title tells us exactly the plot of the novel. <br><br>A man is stabbed to death. He’s a fairly wealthy young man (he’s 21) who runs a cattle ranch inherited from his father. There’s an interesting ethnic twist: His father’s family speaks Arabic at home and his Spanish mother on occasion calls him “white man.” Although the locals call his family ‘Turks,’ because they are Catholic, it’s likely they were Lebanese and arrived in Colombia (where the story is set) from the great mixing and melting pot of the Caribbean. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561468180i/27724037._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The title tells us all we need to know and very early on in the story we know why he was killed, so I’m not giving away any plot. Another wealthy man in town has just had the most extravagant wedding ever seen in the town. But the next morning the bridegroom ‘returns the bride’ to her family because she has been deflowered by the young man who will then be killed by her two brothers. <br><br>The day of the murder the brothers make no secret of their intentions. They wander around town telling people what they intend to do. By mid-morning just about everyone in town has heard them say they will kill him. <br><br>So why is nothing done? Everyone has an excuse. There’s a big commotion in town because the bishop is coming by riverboat to wave at folks and give the town a blessing. So even the priest thinks ‘this is a criminal matter that doesn’t concern me.' Most folks assume it’s just ‘drunk talk.' The mayor has the police chief take away their hog butchering knives when they are seen sharpening them. (But they have plenty of knives.) Also it’s assumed they are talking to so many folks that someone will stop them --- and maybe they want to be stopped.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1561468180i/27724038._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The story is told retrospectively from almost 30 years later. A fascinating read that under the author’s masterful writing, makes the entire story perfectly plausible. “We killed him openly,” Pedro Vicario said, "but we’re innocent.”<br><br>A good read. <br><br>Small town in Colombia from arrangedtravelers.com<br>Photo of the author from npr.com</span>
  <a data-text-id="9569106783632378008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50368738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50368738" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780345310026" href="/book/show/386350.Chronicle_of_a_Death_Foretold">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50368738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1542413865621806575"><b>Crónica de una muerte anunciada = Chronicle of a Death Foretold, Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez</b> <br/><br/>Chronicle of a Death Foretold is a novella by Gabriel García Márquez, published in 1981. It tells, in the form of a pseudo-journalistic reconstruction, the story of the murder of Santiago Nasar by the two Vicario brothers.<br/><br/><b>Characters:</b> Santiago Nasar, Ángela Vicario, Bayardo San Román, Pablo and Pedro Vicario<br/><br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز اول ماه می سال 1994میلادی<br/><br/>عنوان: گزارش یک مرگ، نویسنده: گابریل گارسیا مارکز؛ متر</span>
  <span id="freeText1542413865621806575" style="display:none"><b>Crónica de una muerte anunciada = Chronicle of a Death Foretold, Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez</b> <br /><br />Chronicle of a Death Foretold is a novella by Gabriel García Márquez, published in 1981. It tells, in the form of a pseudo-journalistic reconstruction, the story of the murder of Santiago Nasar by the two Vicario brothers.<br /><br /><b>Characters:</b> Santiago Nasar, Ángela Vicario, Bayardo San Román, Pablo and Pedro Vicario<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز اول ماه می سال 1994میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: گزارش یک مرگ، نویسنده: گابریل گارسیا مارکز؛ مترجم: لیلی گلستان؛ تهران، نشر البرز، چاپ دوم 1362، در 119ص، شابک 9646952643؛ چاپ سوم 1369؛ چاپ چهارم 1370؛ چاپ پنجم 1373؛ چاپ دیگر نشر نو؛ 1361؛ در هشت و 119ص؛ چاپ دیگر نشر ماهی، 1388، در 133ص؛ شابک 9789649971902؛ چاپ دوم 1389؛ چاپ سوم 1390؛ چاپ چهارم و پنجم 1392؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان کلمبیایی - سده 20م<br /><br />روابط سنتی اهالی امریکای لاتین، «مارکز» روزنامه نگار بازهم داستان میسراید؛ یک برداشت است از یک رخداد یا جنایت، شخصیتها و نوع رخداد را تغییر داده، و روایت کرده، نوع روایت بسیار عجیب است؛ هم پیچیده و هم ساده است<br /><br />چکیده داستان: در یک روستای کوچک در نزدیکی دریای «کاراییب» و در یک منطقه ی دور افتاده در «کلمبیا»، «بایاردو سان رومان» ثروتمند و تازه ‌وارد، با «آنخِلا بیکاریو» ازدواج می‌کند، و پس‌از جشن، تازه عروس و داماد، به خانه ی خویش می‌روند؛ در شب زفاف «بایارد» متوجه می‌شود که نو عروس او باکره نبوده، پس از آنکه داماد از این موضوع آگاه می‌شود، به همراه همسرش به خانه ی پدری او می‌روند، و جریان را به آگاهی آن‌ها می‌رساند؛ «آنخلا»، گناهکار را، جوانی به نام «سانتیاگو ناصر» معرفی می‌کند، که از همسایه‌ های خانواده «بیکاریو» در روستا است؛ برادرهای «آنخلا»، «پدرو» و «پابلو»، آماده ی دفاع از آبروی خانواده می‌شوند؛ آن‌ها به اهالی روستا اعلام می‌کنند (برای رهایی از این شرمندگی) به ‌طور یقین «سانتیاگو» را خواهند کشت؛ ولی در آن لحظه هیچکس اقدام کارآمدی برای جلوگیری از کشتن «سانتیاگو» انجام نمی‌دهد، چون بسیاری از روستاییان حرفهای برادران «بیکاریو» را جدی نگرفتند، وعده ‌ای هم خیال می‌کردند، که «سانتیاگو» در جریان موضوع هست، به هر حال دو برادر به سمت خانه ی «سانتیاگو» می‌روند، و او را در کنار در ورودی خانه ی خودش، با چاقو به قتل می‌رسانند، از آن فاجعه بیست و هفت سال می‌گذرد که بهترین دوست «سانتیاگو» که در جریان ماجرا بوده، به بازگویی آن رویداد می‌پردازد<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 12/08/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21189038-chronicle-of-a-death-foretold">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=colombia">colombia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2480999819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1732739185779802088"><i>
  <b>‘’There had never been a death more foretold.’’</b>
</i><br/><br/>Santiago is murdered in the market of a Colombian town. The perpetrators are two brothers. His crime? The supposed defilement of their sister, whose marriage to a young man was broken because she wasn’t a virgin. Márquez does something extraordinary here. He creates one of his finest works, a crime mystery where the question isn’t who or what but why. Why did everything happen in such a way? Why do people have to resort to such actions? Why are </span>
  <span id="freeText1732739185779802088" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>‘’There had never been a death more foretold.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />Santiago is murdered in the market of a Colombian town. The perpetrators are two brothers. His crime? The supposed defilement of their sister, whose marriage to a young man was broken because she wasn’t a virgin. Márquez does something extraordinary here. He creates one of his finest works, a crime mystery where the question isn’t who or what but why. Why did everything happen in such a way? Why do people have to resort to such actions? Why are we worse than the wildest animals? Márquez is concerned with human nature and the origin of truth and lies, In nonlinear narrative, written in first-person account, based on real-life events, Márquez creates a masterpiece whose title entered our everyday speech, whose inevitable violence still shocks us.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’Lowlifes’’ {..} shitty animals that can’t do anything that isn’t something awful.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />When I first read this novella, some 10-odd years ago, I was studying in university and a colleague asked me why on Earth would I ever read a book whose outcome is known beforehand. There is an idiot in every corner and Márquez seemed to know that well. The portraits of the town residents couldn’t have been darker. They condemn Santiago because his origins aren’t the same as their own. He is an Other, therefore he is guilty without the benefit of doubt. Does this ring a bell? Everybody knows what is to come but no one warns him. The police do nothing, the priest forgets the most fundamental of Christ’s teaching and does nothing. When the mob condemns you, you don’t stand a chance. Márquez’s contempt and disgust are evident throughout the novella.<br /><br />Márquez also touches on the themes of gender equality and ‘’purity’’. Santiago isn’t painted in favourable colours, his contempt and violence towards women are often mentioned. Angela is a rather controversial character too. You cannot take a solemn vow whether she speaks the truth or not, she is fickle and empty-headed and as much of a tragic character like Santiago. Was she raped? Was she in love with an other man? These are the actual mysteries of the story.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’I saw the knives in the light from the street lamps and it looked to me that they were dripping blood.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />Márquez is the writer who paved the way for Magical Realism, incorporating its elements to each story in a subtle, poetic way, making the narration flow without turning into Fantasy. This became a common feature in Latin American Literature and quite a few writers tried to mimic him with poor results. In <i>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</i>, the omens acquire an almost Biblical significance. The weather, the animals- primarily dogs- the prophetic dreams bring to mind the fables of the signs that precede a catastrophe. Think of the omens described in Shakespeare’s <i>Julius Caesar</i>, in the night before the vile murder took place.<br /><br />Every book by Márquez requires patience, dedication and a close attention to the story in order to fully understand the wealth and magic of his writing. Márquez is a Latin American Dostoevsky, a writer that should be required reading if we want teenagers to ever become accomplished readers.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’Give me a prejudice and I will move the world.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>

<div id="review_1145750863" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1145750863" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1145750863?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141032467" href="/book/show/2475185.Chronicle_of_a_Death_Foretold">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=top-favs">top-favs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=hispanic">hispanic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1145750863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1948640217318446260"><i>There had never been a death so foretold.</i> <br/><br/>Márquez's oeuvre may be roughly divided into two streams of writing: the magician of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7597.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude' title='One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez' rel='nofollow noopener'>One Hundred Years of Solitude</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9712.Love_in_the_Time_of_Cholera' title='Love in the Time of Cholera by Gabriel García Márquez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Love in the Time of Cholera</a> and the journalist-adventurer of this novel and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16251.News_of_a_Kidnapping' title='News of a Kidnapping by Gabriel García Márquez' rel='nofollow noopener'>News of a Kidnapping</a>. The ominous world of magic realism closes shop when Marquez switches his gears to journalistic storytelling. But may be not quite; because right from the opening scene an eerie premonition trails at the heels of Santiago Nasar and, do what h</span>
  <span id="freeText1948640217318446260" style="display:none"><i>There had never been a death so foretold.</i> <br /><br />Márquez's oeuvre may be roughly divided into two streams of writing: the magician of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7597.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9712.Love_in_the_Time_of_Cholera" title="Love in the Time of Cholera by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Love in the Time of Cholera</a> and the journalist-adventurer of this novel and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16251.News_of_a_Kidnapping" title="News of a Kidnapping by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">News of a Kidnapping</a>. The ominous world of magic realism closes shop when Marquez switches his gears to journalistic storytelling. But may be not quite; because right from the opening scene an eerie premonition trails at the heels of Santiago Nasar and, do what he might, catches him unawares to punish him for a crime he might or might not have committed. In this novella the writer-narrator sets himself up to the task of investigating the events that led to the <i>death so foretold.</i><br /><br />You may call it a reportage of an impossible and inexplicable murder that could have been prevented with just a shout. You may call it story of a man who must pay for violating the unwritten code of honour when he is suspected of deflowering Angela Vicario, his best pal's sister, hours before she was to get married to another man. Or you may simply call it a story of an honour killing, but unlike other stories of this kind this is not yet another banal attempt aimed at soliciting public's disgust at the horrific practice, told from the moral high ground of an observer's point of view. It does not portray the entire value system backward and barbaric which stories of this kind are wont to do, but operates from inside the culture to report on the dynamics that lead to and make something like this possible. This objectivity sits at the heart of good writing and this sets Márquez apart from a bevy of writers who have produced stereotypical fictional accounts of a culture at war with itself.<br /><br />I won't call it "unreliable narration" but rather a deliberate building up of ambiguity with respect to the victim's role: Was Santiago Nasar guilty of dishonouring Angela Vicario, or was there a big misunderstanding all along, made all the more complicated by a surfeit of circumstantial evidence? Here you have Márquez, the master investigative journalist, with the best implements of his trade, testing your powers of observation and turning you into a witness who is being strung along willy-nilly by a powerful voice. It's one of those novels where the process of telling a story is greater than the story itself. In other words, how it's told rather than what's told.<br /><br />By the honour of Angela Vicario this is a story unequaled in its telling!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1948640217318446260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1745543146" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1745543146" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1745543146?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1745543146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2436430977745404155">Nobel Laureate Gabriel Garcia Marquez who is best known for his One Hundred Years of Solitude penned a novella A Chronicle of Death Foretold based on real life events that he witnessed. In this novella, Marquez tells through the eyes of an unnamed narrator the events that lead to the murder of Santiago Nasar. It is in the characterizations of the personas this short book that we get a taste of Marquez' brilliance which won him many honors during his writing career. <br/><br/>Santiago Nasar is the only chi</span>
  <span id="freeText2436430977745404155" style="display:none">Nobel Laureate Gabriel Garcia Marquez who is best known for his One Hundred Years of Solitude penned a novella A Chronicle of Death Foretold based on real life events that he witnessed. In this novella, Marquez tells through the eyes of an unnamed narrator the events that lead to the murder of Santiago Nasar. It is in the characterizations of the personas this short book that we get a taste of Marquez' brilliance which won him many honors during his writing career. <br /><br />Santiago Nasar is the only child of Ibrahim Nasar and Placida Nacido. He is engaged to be married to Flor Miguel, but as his father before him, has engaged in trysts with his housekeeper's daughter. Likewise he has frequented a whorehouse and has an intimate relationship with its matron. <br /><br />Meanwhile Bayardo San Ramon and Angela Vicario are to be married. Angela is not a virgin and San Ramon kicks her out of their marriage chamber. She is dishonored by her mother, and her twin brothers Pablo and Pedro are determined to kill the man responsible for their sister's reckless behavior. All signs point to Santiago Nasar. <br /><br />The narrator tells this tale when all principal players have reached middle age. A boyhood friend of Nasar and a cousin of the Vicarios, he is determined to find out if Nasar was really guilty. Angela has paid the price, living like an old maid, yet still professing her love to Bayardo San Ramon after all these years. Yet despite the narrator's attempts to piece all the evidence together, Angela will not reveal the key clue, leaving the reader hanging throughout this short tale. <br /><br />Even in this short tale, it is obvious to me that Garcia Marquez knows how to weave a story together. I am fan of Isabel Allende and people tell me that her writing reminds them of Garcia Marquez. As this is my first taste of his writing, I can sense the similarities- many characters in a Latin American village, the opening sentences containing flashbacks, and a high level of magical realism. I rate this novella 4 stars for its short length as I look forward to reading Garcia Marquez' opus which earned him international renown.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2436430977745404155" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21189038-chronicle-of-a-death-foretold">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20438238-mutasim-billah?shelf=colombia">colombia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2304826773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7349058037545837471"><blockquote>
  <i>"On the day they were going to kill him, Santiago Nasar got up at five-thirty in the morning to wait for the boat the bishop was coming on."</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>A grand wedding. A gruesome murder. And a multitude of lives changed forever. <i>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</i> is a novella which recounts a fictionalized account inspired by an actual incident of honor-killing in Colombia back in 1951, now immortalized in this 1981 novella.<br/><br/>The story is about the series of events leading up to a murder, and its aftermath. I</span>
  <span id="freeText7349058037545837471" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"On the day they were going to kill him, Santiago Nasar got up at five-thirty in the morning to wait for the boat the bishop was coming on."</i>
</blockquote><br><br>A grand wedding. A gruesome murder. And a multitude of lives changed forever. <i>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</i> is a novella which recounts a fictionalized account inspired by an actual incident of honor-killing in Colombia back in 1951, now immortalized in this 1981 novella.<br><br>The story is about the series of events leading up to a murder, and its aftermath. It is written in pseudo-journalistic style in the form of interviewed accounts staged 20 years after the tragedy. The more fantastic aspects of the novel are that the victim is made to be a Jesus-like figure, so much so that his death is almost likened to crucifixion. One can also relate to the theatrics of the death of Julius Caesar while reading this story.<br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1591724323i/29620187._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  
    <i>The death of Julius Caesar</i>
  
  <br>
<br><b>Fun fact</b>: The novel led to a <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2011/nov/30/gabriel-garcia-marquez-court-victory" rel="nofollow noopener">17-year legal battle</a> after a man claimed unlawful use of his life story for the novel.</span>

  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1897944852" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1897944852" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1897944852?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1897944852" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3671647010572625580">Stabbing a man to death is not as easy as it sounds, after all, it took 23 swipes at poor old Julius Caesar, but only one of them was actually deemed fatal. It takes a hell of an effort. <br/>Not like that seen in those pathetic slasher movies, where a big breasted peroxide blonde is chased around a mansion by a nutcase bearing a huge blade that only dishes out flesh wounds.<br/><br/>Chronicling the murder of one Santiago Nasar in a small unnamed South American village, Márquez dazzles in a fictional world tha</span>
  <span id="freeText3671647010572625580" style="display:none">Stabbing a man to death is not as easy as it sounds, after all, it took 23 swipes at poor old Julius Caesar, but only one of them was actually deemed fatal. It takes a hell of an effort. <br />Not like that seen in those pathetic slasher movies, where a big breasted peroxide blonde is chased around a mansion by a nutcase bearing a huge blade that only dishes out flesh wounds.<br /><br />Chronicling the murder of one Santiago Nasar in a small unnamed South American village, Márquez dazzles in a fictional world that is as pungently sharp as the knives used to kill, it's a murder everyone in town knew was coming, yet no one wanted. ''Never was a death more foretold.'' says the the novels narrator, recounting the events of that fateful day, in fact, it's many years later where the story is told from, using the recollections of various townsfolk, a picture is slowly built up of just "who" and "why" this horrendous act took place. The purpose is less to do with trying solve any mystery, as the deed was committed in broad daylight, by two brothers, who understood their sister had been dishonored by Nasar, it's more based on the determination to try and understand why no one was able to prevent the death from happening. Spare in tone and somewhat bizarre it's a clever piece of writing that explores the nature of complicity and fate, and of how an act of savage intent can effect so many lives over time. <br /><br />Márquez utilizes mysticism and spirituality in his writing that represents culture, and symbolizes meaning within the context of the work. The death of Santiago may not have been avoided, partially due to the strange inabilities of his characters to connect with their spiritual powers. Both Santiago’s mother and his god-mother are unable to ‘truly feel his demise’. Mostly random thoughts and actions of several different people contribute to altering the course of that murderous day, with the fate of Santiago totally left in the hands of others.<br />Although a quick read, what I admired the most was the fact it's a deeper story than you think, it's not just the death of one, but the suffering of many, those who are burdened with failed responsibilities, the knives may have been in the hands of the killers, but seemingly the whole village also has a tight grip around them, this makes the story all the more powerful and ultimately<br />sad in the end, leaving an open wound on the people, unlikely ever to heal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3671647010572625580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10325776699437501155">I read this short masterpiece <i>ages</i> ago, but it only recently resurfaced in my mind, like a forgotten dream. It's a tense drama of murder foretold, written in the magical realism style for which Columbian novelist, Marquez, was lionised.<br/>I really need to read it again to reacquaint myself with its succulent excellence.<br/>(Ditto, Don Quixote, and so many other exquisitely-written novels that are now consigned to the backroom of my cluttered mind).</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jokoloyo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jokoloyo" href="/user/show/1902630-jokoloyo">Jokoloyo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1838902061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17088965032815176803">Before I started reading this book, Goodreads have already recorded 73,000 ratings and 3,500 reviews. <br/><br/>When I was reading this book, I found out some friends had read this book at his/her school age. How lucky you are, my friends. This is a good novel.<br/><br/>There are thousands <i>correct</i> ways to write a story. This novel used one of the rarely used ways. It was using non-linear timeline story, move back-and-forth between multi POV from a same morning when the incident occurred. It was not the first story </span>
  <span id="freeText17088965032815176803" style="display:none">Before I started reading this book, Goodreads have already recorded 73,000 ratings and 3,500 reviews. <br /><br />When I was reading this book, I found out some friends had read this book at his/her school age. How lucky you are, my friends. This is a good novel.<br /><br />There are thousands <i>correct</i> ways to write a story. This novel used one of the rarely used ways. It was using non-linear timeline story, move back-and-forth between multi POV from a same morning when the incident occurred. It was not the first story using it ("In The Groove" of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/672376.Rashomon_and_Other_Stories" title="Rashomon and Other Stories by Ryūnosuke Akutagawa" rel="nofollow noopener">Rashomon and Other Stories</a> came to my mind), but Márquez added more factors into the story: unavoidable fate (no matter how good your intentions or how hard your efforts), or how some persons were weak-willed too afraid to intervene. In other words, this fiction injected some realistic factors in the novel, combined with skillful writing.<br /><br />I want to point the HAPPY ending for this novel. How genius Márquez put the real end of the story in the middle of the story <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[at the end of part 4. I have re-read those a few last paragraphs of part 4, even in the middle of writing this review <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It was <b>so beautiful</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[romance/relationship/love story where the older Bayardo San Roman came to Angela Vicario, bringing unopened Angela's two thousands letters <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17088965032815176803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_669451945" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/669451945" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/669451945?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=mwahaha-gore">mwahaha-gore</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=psychological">psychological</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=2013">2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer669451945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10170147824126195553"><a href='https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png' width='350' height='117' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>I can't believe I had not written a review for this book. Since I'm constantly recommending it to people, I <i>should</i> have written a review at some point... but I didn't. Now is the time when I try to do it justice with this, as it is one of my fave books.<br/><br/><b>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</b>... that's exactly what this book is about: A death foretold. You may be asking yourselves, wtf <i>is</i> that? Well, that was pretty much my reaction in 2013 when I read this for the first time.<br/><br/>Santiago Nasar is found de</span>
  <span id="freeText10170147824126195553" style="display:none"><a href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" width="350" height="117" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>I can't believe I had not written a review for this book. Since I'm constantly recommending it to people, I <i>should</i> have written a review at some point... but I didn't. Now is the time when I try to do it justice with this, as it is one of my fave books.<br><br><b>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</b>... that's exactly what this book is about: A death foretold. You may be asking yourselves, wtf <i>is</i> that? Well, that was pretty much my reaction in 2013 when I read this for the first time.<br><br>Santiago Nasar is found dead. He has been stabbed multiple times by a pair of twins (Pablo and Pedro Vicario), but really, that's nothing unusually remarkable - happens every day. What about his death makes it so impressive? <b>The fact that the twins told EVERYONE they were going to kill him.</b><br><br>Funny thing is NO ONE believed them, as they were supposedly the role model of the neighborhood - they would never do such a thing! That's just a joke! And btw, why would they even tell they're planning a murder? Yep, definitely a joke.<br><br>Turns out they were speaking seriously.<br><br>And <i>that's</i> why it is a <b>chronicle of a death foretold</b>.<br><br>The story is narrated through interviews to people who were related to Santiago in some way - be it directly or indirectly. It follows the events that led to the death: How it happened, <i>why</i> they did it, and why no one did anything... or why they actually tried to do something when it was already too late.<br><br>This book belongs to magical realism: What happens in it are things that theoretically are normal in real life, but they are so ridiculously improbable of actually happening that they are "magical." Why, you say? Well, this is not fantasy, obviously, but when you're reading the novel, you're always thinking, "please, this is NOT realistic. If someone was threatening to kill another person, people WOULD do something. They would not stay quiet."<br><br>Yeah, I agree with that, since that was pretty much me when I read this for the first (and now second) time. But the thing is that the more you read, the more sense the things make. <b>Improbable things suddenly become common and the usual becomes rare.</b><br><br>That's the effect this writer provokes.<br><br>Not only did he do that to me, but also the writing is so good (I read the original version in Spanish, so I don't know how the translations are) it grips you. He does not hide any of the facts and it's SHOWING, not telling – meaning the kind of writing we all love.<br><br>Characterization is excellent too, even when you never know the characters by some kind of narrator telling you how they think. As I said, the book is written in interviews format, so all you know about everyone is what the author of the article for the newspaper knows, including his own opinions about them.<br><br>I am really aware I am babbling right now, but I just wish you could just give this book a try. It's one of those classics that are worth it. It managed to approve the test of the 2015 rereads, which if I remember correctly, has just been approved by one series of all the books I reread.<br><br><i>Anyway</i>, please, please, PLEASE add this book. If you're a fan of magical realism, you should read this too. I know latin-american authors are not very popular, but at least give this one a try. He really deserves your reading time.<br><br>I have not read anything else by this author, but I sure will. My mother has recommended me <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9712.Love_in_the_Time_of_Cholera" title="Love in the Time of Cholera by Gabriel Garcí­a Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">Love in the Time of Cholera</a>... and I have plans of reading another one of his books. If you have read and liked one of those books, then this is just for you; if you didn't like them, then ehhhh; BUT if you're a newbie, I repeat: READ THIS.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10170147824126195553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12122332584968443377">There’s a kind of cheap thrill to dishing out one star to a Nobel prize winner and a guy I previously gave 5 stars to for the brilliant One Hundred Years of Solitude, but it has to be done because on a sentence by sentence level this this thin story in a thin book is as dull as ditchwater which has lost the will to live, all about some honor killing but of a guy not a woman, the alleged deflowerer of a returned bride.   <span id="freeText12122332584968443377" style="display:none">There’s a kind of cheap thrill to dishing out one star to a Nobel prize winner and a guy I previously gave 5 stars to for the brilliant One Hundred Years of Solitude, but it has to be done because on a sentence by sentence level this this thin story in a thin book is as dull as ditchwater which has lost the will to live, all about some honor killing but of a guy not a woman, the alleged deflowerer of a returned bride. Yeah, we are in a society where if the bride isn’t a virgin she’s returned – “this one’s secondhand, I ordered new, I want my money back”, so the brothers of the unfortunately-all-too-consummated sister go and shoot the alleged deflowerer. I didn’t care who shot who or how many chickens were cooked or how or what the dress looked like or why or how many complicated street festivals there were for who knew what obscure saints and who saw who do what at 3.32 on the fateful afternoon or which sisters of which family were able to levitate and how many times the brothers told everybody and his uncle and his uncle’s uncle that they were going to shoot Santiago Nasar and when and how and where and which and why nobody called the cops and all this and that. But that's just me, I hope you like it.</span>

<div id="review_84760146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84760146" />
    <a title="Traveller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller"><img alt="Traveller" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423246742p2/3145526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84760146?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Traveller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Traveller" href="/user/show/3145526-traveller">Traveller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3145526-traveller?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84760146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15203483175678015835">Brilliantly told....<br/><br/>I've just re-read this novel; and I've just remembered why it stuck with me after reading it the first time. <br/><br/>I have since learned that the novella is based on a true story, in which the author himself had been involved. This caused the book to carry even more of a disturbing impact for me.<br/><br/>The novel (novella? it's rather short) starts off in detective/journalistic investigative fashion; at first it seems casual and desultory; the narrator seems to be merely reporting. However,</span>
  <span id="freeText15203483175678015835" style="display:none">Brilliantly told....<br /><br />I've just re-read this novel; and I've just remembered why it stuck with me after reading it the first time. <br /><br />I have since learned that the novella is based on a true story, in which the author himself had been involved. This caused the book to carry even more of a disturbing impact for me.<br /><br />The novel (novella? it's rather short) starts off in detective/journalistic investigative fashion; at first it seems casual and desultory; the narrator seems to be merely reporting. However, in spite of the text having the discipline and surface appearance of a journalistic style, when you realise that the author himself was emotionally impacted by the events, it dawns on you that the author has been attempting to work through his own horror and grief and helplessness at the events described in the book. The disciplined style in which he narrates, and the way in which he arranges the seemingly eclectic (but upon reflection obviously thoughtfully arranged) recollections, adds to the haunting and reflective qualities of the narration. <br /><br />It is only at the very end of the book that the author exposes one to the more visceral horror of the events, and here the journalistic, almost dispassionate narrative style serves to very effectively portray the horror inherent in this death that was so thoroughly foretold.<br /><br />The material has been so cleverly arranged that the narration picks up in emotional impact as the "investigation" develops, and the story finishes off with a visceral climax that leaves you feeling as if you had been punched in the solar plexus.<br /><br />It is a brilliant look at a set of events that was precipitated not only by acts, but by a certain mindset, - a single sexual act ended up affecting an entire town, and the effects could still be felt twenty seven years later.<br /><br />At the start of his investigation the author sets out to seek answers as to the "why" the death happened. Throughout the book he presents a thousand "if only's"; - of instances of how the death could have been avoided if only this or that had happened slightly differently. <br /><br />The townspeople seem to believe it was a fait accompli because it was either ordained by fate, or, since it was a deed of "honour" within a certain religious mindset, it "had to" be done. <br /><br />I get the feeling that these are not the sentiments of the author himself though, and that at the end, he is left with the same rage of helpless incomprehension; which is yet subtly laced with a certain fatalistic acceptance, that he leaves the reader with. The most ironic twist for me was that it was the victims' own mother, who, at the very end, while actually trying to save her son, unwittingly finally sealed his fate.<br /><br />PS. It would seem that whomever did the Goodreads description introducing the novel, got hold of the wrong plot. It doesn't describe quite the correct set of events that take place in the novel, or even the actual POV of the novel, but oh, well...</span>
  <a data-text-id="15203483175678015835" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_717086091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/717086091" />

              <span id="reviewTextContainer717086091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18093788201198532769">It's possible that Gabriel Garcia Marquez's grocery list is better written than most of what stands on your bookshelves.<br/><br/>What Marquez does feels less like writing and more like the conveyance of a mood or a dream that imprints itself on paper as you read, then disappears behind you.<br/><br/>It's hard to find fault with him as a writer.<br/><br/>This is a particularly fascinating "skinny read," a little book with a lot of bang for your buck. <br/><br/>It is highly literary and also incredibly thought-provoking. I highly recom</span>
  <span id="freeText18093788201198532769" style="display:none">It's possible that Gabriel Garcia Marquez's grocery list is better written than most of what stands on your bookshelves.<br /><br />What Marquez does feels less like writing and more like the conveyance of a mood or a dream that imprints itself on paper as you read, then disappears behind you.<br /><br />It's hard to find fault with him as a writer.<br /><br />This is a particularly fascinating "skinny read," a little book with a lot of bang for your buck. <br /><br />It is highly literary and also incredibly thought-provoking. I highly recommend it, especially for book clubs and advanced classrooms.</span>

<div id="review_1774019563" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1774019563" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1774019563?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">mystery lovers seeking a short thrill</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=latin-america">latin-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=short-shorts">short-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fav-authors">fav-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1774019563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2365903964649689270">I should start by confessing how Márquez's words have often flirted with me and I've fallen for his elongated, somewhat eccentric, prose style. In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23887.The_Autumn_of_the_Patriarch' title='The Autumn of the Patriarch by Gabriel García Márquez' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Autumn of the Patriarch</a> he took me on a satisfying and bewildering journey that secretly made me question his sanity. So I've come to expect that part-dizzying, part-dazzling style that makes this short work feel different, almost as if it was written by another Márquez, maybe a more controlled Márquez, but certainly not the same one who wrote <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude' title='One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez' rel='nofollow noopener'>One</a></span>
  <span id="freeText2365903964649689270" style="display:none">I should start by confessing how Márquez's words have often flirted with me and I've fallen for his elongated, somewhat eccentric, prose style. In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23887.The_Autumn_of_the_Patriarch" title="The Autumn of the Patriarch by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">The Autumn of the Patriarch</a> he took me on a satisfying and bewildering journey that secretly made me question his sanity. So I've come to expect that part-dizzying, part-dazzling style that makes this short work feel different, almost as if it was written by another Márquez, maybe a more controlled Márquez, but certainly not the same one who wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/320.One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude by Gabriel García Márquez" rel="nofollow noopener">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a>. <br /><br />Strangely, this book is more Márquez than the others. He had a friend who was murdered because he supposedly "deflowered" a woman (there are so many thoughts running through my head with the oddity of this, but I won't linger on the inhumanity of people). Since the victim's mother was a close friend, Márquez's mother didn't want him to use his journalistic skills and conduct narrative nonfiction or investigative reporting. In the end, he decided to explore his grief and conundrum through this work of fiction. <br /><br />I can't remember a book I've read where in the first paragraph, the author tells me the character will be murdered, and yet there is still tension to drive the rest of the scene for me. With the complex and circular story structure, one is gracefully introduced to the dead Santiago Nasar, one nods at his killers, one meets the strange and mysterious groom,Bayardo San Roman, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(who as it turns out is not really mysterious after all, maybe only an outsider searching for love) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but one never truly understands Angela Vicario, the woman at the center of it all. <br /><b>
  <blockquote>She only took the time necessary to say the name. She looked for it in the shadows, she found it at first sight among the many, many easily confused names from this world and the other, and she nailed it to the wall with her well-aimed dart, like a butterfly with no will whose sentence has always been written.</blockquote>
</b><br />Sure, the mystery is never truly solved because in real life, there was always lingering doubts. But storytelling maneuverability is something one can always lean on in a Márquez novel. I enjoyed reading this story that manipulates time and place, where characters are developed in such artful ways that the years slip by within two to five pages and I'm so enchanted that I don't notice how much these characters have changed, until later. Yet there is so much to be saddened by, so many people to feel pity for; each character seems to be the victim of the kind of conformity that strangles and butchers and turns good into evil. As in most of his novels, Márquez investigates the ways of an entire community, a culture, a country; he elucidates absurdities that lurk in common ways of thinking and the story seems to not only seek answers for the crime, but answers to the bafflement of complacency that allows murder without someone having the decency to say <i>enough</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2365903964649689270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3221841227" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3221841227" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3221841227?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21189038-chronicle-of-a-death-foretold">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=colombia">colombia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-1980s">decade-1980s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=latin-american">latin-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=hispanosphere">hispanosphere</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=nobel">nobel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3221841227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4921709364025955800">It's around 25 years since I last read a whole book by Márquez - the only one before this - yet his style, and what made it characteristically his rather than that of countless imitators, felt as familiar, <i>too</i> familiar, even, as if I'd read half a dozen of his books. Perhaps that's how the imitations agglomerate, or I suspect, because the novels one reads as a teenager imprint most strongly. Back then I didn't quite see what all the fuss was about, and I still don't, except that it's worth readi</span>
  <span id="freeText4921709364025955800" style="display:none">It's around 25 years since I last read a whole book by Márquez - the only one before this - yet his style, and what made it characteristically his rather than that of countless imitators, felt as familiar, <i>too</i> familiar, even, as if I'd read half a dozen of his books. Perhaps that's how the imitations agglomerate, or I suspect, because the novels one reads as a teenager imprint most strongly. Back then I didn't quite see what all the fuss was about, and I still don't, except that it's worth reading him to understand his influence on other authors.<br /><br />Contemporary novels influenced by Márquez have been backing up in my reading list for a while now, waiting for me to read or re-read him so I get more out of them. (And at my age and given the sort of stuff I read, I should be more familiar with his work.) Last year this bothered me when I was on a bloggers' shadowing group for the International Booker - and again this year it has come up. Longlisted Mexican novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/51969760.Hurricane_Season" title="Hurricane Season by Fernanda Melchor" rel="nofollow noopener">Hurricane Season</a> by Fernanda Melchor is reportedly structured after <i>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</i>. (Plus, another book on the list, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35708940.The_Enlightenment_of_the_Greengage_Tree" title="The Enlightenment of the Greengage Tree by Shokoofeh Azar" rel="nofollow noopener">The Enlightenment of the Greengage Tree</a> has been repeatedly compared to Márquez in reviews.) As <i>Chronicle</i> is so short - unlike a re-read of <i>100 Years of Solitude</i> - I decided to read it before either of those two, and therefore finally get round to reading Márquez again. <br /><br />So here it was, that meshing of the whimsical and the fated; those longish curling sentences of Márquez-via-Rabassa about people doing serious things, or having them done to them, described in a way which combines the quirky and the biblical. A smidgen too light, but on the other hand it would be more difficult to handle if it weren't. I found myself thinking a lot about Wes Anderson, especially his less deft films which ignore or gloss over subjects that are important to their settings (e.g. <i>The Darjeeling Limited</i>.) Because if there's any take on a major author which I'd not previously heard, and that Goodreads has bombarded me with repeatedly over the last eight years, it's criticism of Gabriel García Márquez's questionable sexual politics. Even when I was still quite resistant to identity politics, some of his work just sounded a bit too dodgy because of this (e.g. <i>Memories of My Melancholy Whores</i>) and I decided not to bother with him. But he's also kind of essential to Latin American literature, and there are novels which critique his. (It sounds like <i>Hurricane Season</i> may be one: practically everybody in the locality is implicated in a murder rooted in patriarchal values, as in <i>Chronicle</i> - but while in Márquez's book, the victim is a rich young man, in Melchor's it is a subversive woman, re-centring the adverse effects where they often fall hardest in reality. <i>Chronicle</i> is based on a real killing from 1951; Melchor is probably saying, all too justifiably given the horrendous situation with femicides in Mexico, that too little has changed in the region's attitudes to women in nearly 70 years.)<br /><br /><i>Chronicle of a Death Foretold</i> is all about responsibility on an individual or small-community level: the oversights and accidents which meant nobody directly told Santiago Nasar that the Vicario brothers were out to kill him, until it was too late, and the failure by those with authority to recognise Pablo and Pedro - not gangsters as it would be easy to assume from the early pages, but average young working men in their village - were serious about it, not just talking with drunken bravado. If Santiago Nasar wasn't actually the first sexual partner of Angela Vicario - the murderers' sister who had just been rejected by her rich bridegroom on their wedding night - who was it? These are the issues that ostensibly matter in the novel. (It's never asked whether Angela consented the first time she had sex.) The Vicario brothers do seem to have hoped, on some level, that they would actually be stopped from killing Nasar, and it's strongly implied that killing the man in question, whoever he was, was excessive. But it's less clear (and now, more than in 1981, when <i>Chronicle</i> was first published, more readers <i>want</i> novels to be clear about such attitudes) whether - although the novel shows fallout from the overvaluing of virginity in women, and of traditional, inaccurate thinking about hymens - it is critical of the complex of patriarchal values and customs that lead to Angela's abandonment, Santiago's murder and bridegroom Bayardo's phase of alcoholism.<br /><br />In <i>Chronicle</i> there are also tropes common in older literary or classic fiction, which are often criticised these days. The narrator and other men routinely have sex with prostitutes and it's a normal part of life for them. A male character met his future wife while he was an adult and she was still a child. (He jokingly proposes to her when he is about 20 and she at primary school, though they don't marry until 14 years later.) Obsessively and persistently contacting a love interest who appears uninterested, in this case over many years, is eventually rewarded with a positive response. (It was not in reality.) <br /><br />For some, the dubious sexual politics will be reason not to read the book at all, or to give it a low rating. Others may care about these points to an extent, whilst the writing/translation and structure elevate the novel regardless. It could also be seen as an interesting examination of behaviour and motivations in an honour culture where modern Western values have only a partial presence.<br /><br />Anyway, this is another Latin American classic about which it's obvious why a feminist author would write a response or retelling - also on the longlist is <i>The Adventures of China Iron</i>, narrated by the young wife of <i>Martín Fierro</i>, hero of Argentina's foundational gaucho epic.<br /><br />I wouldn't necessarily expect a novel from 1981, and set decades earlier, to fit with contemporary left-leaning values, and ultimately it's Márquez's style - writing which has bowled over countless fans - that leaves me a bit meh. I mean, it's alright, I don't mind it so much that it would put me off reading more by him, but it doesn't leave me raring to, either. His characters often seem like romanticised archetypes more than richly-drawn individuals; there are books I really love which do this, but Márquez's are, so far, not among them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4921709364025955800" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
